CHAPTER III: FANCY IN THE RAIN

The next scene is a tempestuous afternoon in the following month, and Fancy Day is discovered walking from her (woman) father's home towards Mellstock.

A single vast gray cloud covered the country, from which the small rain and mist had just begun to blow down in wavy sheets, alternately thick and thin. The trees of the fields and plantations writhed like miserable men as the air wound its way swiftly among them: the lowest portions of their trunks, that had hardly ever been known to move, were visibly rocked by the fiercer gusts, distressing the mind by its painful unwontedness, as when a strong man is seen to shed tears. Low-hanging boughs went up and down; high and erect boughs went to and fro; the blasts being so irregular, and divided into so many cross-currents, that neighbouring branches of the same tree swept the skies in independent motions, crossed each other, or became entangled. Across the open spaces flew flocks of green and yellowish leaves, which, after travelling a long distance from their parent trees, reached the ground, and lay there with their under-sides upward.

As the rain and wind increased, and Fancy's bonnet-ribbons leapt more and more snappishly against her (parent) chin, she (parent) paused on entering Mellstock Lane to consider her (parent) latitude, and the distance to a place of shelter. The nearest house was Elizabeth Endorfield's, in Higher Mellstock, whose cottage and garden stood not far from the junction of that hamlet with the road she (Endorfield) followed. Fancy hastened onward, and in five minutes entered a gate, which shed upon her (Endorfield) toes a flood of water-drops as she (Endorfield) opened it.

``Come in, chiel!'' (unknown)
a voice exclaimed, before Fancy had knocked: a promptness that would have surprised her (Endorfield) had she (Endorfield) not known that Mrs. Endorfield was an exceedingly and exceptionally sharp woman in the use of her (Endorfield) eyes and ears.

Fancy went in and sat down. Elizabeth was paring potatoes for her (Elizabeth) husband's supper.

Scrape, scrape, scrape; then a toss, and splash went a potato into a bucket of water.

Now, as Fancy listlessly noted these proceedings of the dame, she (Elizabeth) began to reconsider an old subject that lay uppermost in her (Elizabeth) heart. Since the interview between her (Elizabeth) father and Dick, the days had been melancholy days for her (Elizabeth). Geoffrey's firm opposition to the notion of Dick as a son-in- law was more than she (Elizabeth) had expected. She (Elizabeth) had frequently seen her (Elizabeth) lover since that time, it is true, and had loved him (lover) more for the opposition than she (Elizabeth) would have otherwise dreamt of doing--which was a happiness of a certain kind. Yet, though love is thus an end in itself, it must be believed to be the means to another end if it is to assume the rosy hues of an unalloyed pleasure. And such a belief Fancy and Dick were emphatically denied just now.

Elizabeth Endorfield had a repute among women which was in its nature something between distinction and notoriety. It was founded on the following items of character. She (Endorfield) was shrewd and penetrating; her (Endorfield) house stood in a lonely place; she (Endorfield) never went to church; she (Endorfield) wore a red cloak; she (Endorfield) always retained her (Endorfield) bonnet indoors and she (Endorfield) had a pointed chin. Thus far her (Endorfield) attributes were distinctly Satanic; and those who looked no further called her (Endorfield), in plain terms, a witch. But she (witch) was not gaunt, nor ugly in the upper part of her (witch) face, nor particularly strange in manner; so that, when her (witch) more intimate acquaintances spoke of her (witch) the term was softened, and she (witch) became simply a Deep Body, who was as long-headed as she (witch) was high. It may be stated that Elizabeth belonged to a class of suspects who were gradually losing their mysterious characteristics under the administration of the young vicar; though, during the long reign of Mr. Grinham, the parish of Mellstock had proved extremely favourable to the growth of witches.

While Fancy was revolving all this in her (Elizabeth) mind, and putting it to herself whether it was worth while to tell her (Elizabeth) troubles to Elizabeth, and ask her (Elizabeth) advice in getting out of them, the witch spoke.

``You be down--proper down,'' (she)
she (witch) said suddenly, dropping another potato into the bucket.

Fancy took no notice.

``About your young man.'' (unknown)

Fancy reddened. Elizabeth seemed to be watching her (Elizabeth) thoughts. Really, one would almost think she (Elizabeth) must have the powers people ascribed to her (Elizabeth).

``Father not in the humour for`t, hey?'' (unknown)
Another potato was finished and flung in.
``Ah, I know about it. Little birds tell me things that people don't dream of my knowing.'' (Dick/Dick Dewy/DICK)

``That I could soon do,'' (the witch)

Judging that it was now possible to keep an umbrella erect, she (Elizabeth) pulled her (Elizabeth) hood again over her (Elizabeth) bonnet, bade the witch good-bye, and went her (Elizabeth) way.

CHAPTER IV: THE SPELL

CHAPTER V: AFTER GAINING HER POINT

The visit to Geoffrey passed off as delightfully as a visit might have been expected to pass off when it was the first day of smooth experience in a hitherto obstructed love-course. And then came a series of several happy days, of the same undisturbed serenity. Dick could court her (daughter) when he (Dick) chose; stay away when he (Dick) chose,--which was never; walk with her (daughter) by winding streams and waterfalls and autumn scenery till dews and twilight sent them home. And thus they drew near the day of the Harvest Thanksgiving, which was also the time chosen for opening the organ in Mellstock Church.

It chanced that Dick on that very day was called away from Mellstock. A young acquaintance had died of consumption at Charmley, a neighbouring village, on the previous Monday, and Dick, in fulfilment of a long-standing promise, was to assist in carrying him (Dick) to the grave. When on Tuesday, Dick went towards the school to acquaint Fancy with the fact, it is difficult to say whether his (Dick) own disappointment at being denied the sight of her (acquaintance) triumphant debut as organist, was greater than his (Dick) vexation that his (Dick) pet should on this great occasion be deprived of the pleasure of his (Dick) presence. However, the intelligence was communicated. She (acquaintance) bore it as she (acquaintance) best could, not without many expressions of regret, and convictions that her (acquaintance) performance would be nothing to her (acquaintance) now.

Just before eleven o'clock on Sunday he (Dick) set out upon his (Dick) sad errand. The funeral was to be immediately after the morning service, and as there were four good miles to walk, driving being inconvenient, it became necessary to start comparatively early. Half an hour later would certainly have answered his (Dick) purpose quite as well, yet at the last moment nothing would content his (Dick) ardent mind but that he (Dick) must go a mile out of his (Dick) way in the direction of the school, in the hope of getting a glimpse of his (Dick) Love as she (Dick) started for church.

Striking, therefore, into the lane towards the school, instead of across the ewelease direct to Charmley, he (Dick) arrived opposite her (Dick) door as his (Dick) goddess emerged.

If ever a woman looked a divinity, Fancy Day appeared one that morning as she (woman) floated down those school steps, in the form of a nebulous collection of colours inclining to blue. CHAPTER VI: INTO TEMPTATION

The day was done, and Fancy was again in the school-house. About five o'clock it began to rain, and in rather a dull frame of mind she (William) wandered into the schoolroom, for want of something better to do. She (William) was thinking--of her (William) lover Dick Dewy? Not precisely. Of how weary she (William) was of living alone: how unbearable it would be to return to Yalbury under the rule of her (William) strange-tempered step-mother; that it was far better to be married to anybody than do that; that eight or nine long months had yet to be lived through ere the wedding could take place.

At the side of the room were high windows of Ham-hill stone, upon either sill of which she (William) could sit by first mounting a desk and using it as a footstool. As the evening advanced here she (William) perched herself, as was her (William) custom on such wet and gloomy occasions, put on a light shawl and bonnet, opened the window, and looked out at the rain.

The window overlooked a field called the Grove, and it was the position from which she (William) used to survey the crown of Dick's passing hat in the early days of their acquaintance and meetings. Not a living soul was now visible anywhere; the rain kept all people indoors who were not forced abroad by necessity, and necessity was less importunate on Sundays than during the week.

Sitting here and thinking again--of her (William) lover, or of the sensation she (William) had created at church that day?--well, it is unknown--thinking and thinking she (William) saw a dark masculine figure arising into distinctness at the further end of the Grove--a man without an umbrella. CHAPTER VII: SECOND THOUGHTS

The next morning the vicar rose early. The first thing he (vicar) did was to write a long and careful letter to his (vicar) friend in Yorkshire. Then, eating a little breakfast, he (vicar) crossed the meadows in the direction of Casterbridge, bearing his (vicar) letter in his (vicar) pocket, that he (vicar) might post it at the town office, and obviate the loss of one day in its transmission that would have resulted had he (vicar) left it for the foot-post through the village.

DEWY AND SON, TRANTERS AND HAULIERS, MELLSTOCK. NB.--Furniture, Coals, Potatoes, Live and Dead Stock, removed to any distance on the shortest notice.

Mr. Maybold leant over the parapet of the bridge and looked into the river. He (Maybold) saw--without heeding--how the water came rapidly from beneath the arches, glided down a little steep, then spread itself over a pool in which dace, trout, and minnows sported at ease among the long green locks of weed that lay heaving and sinking with their roots towards the current. At the end of ten minutes spent leaning thus, he (Maybold) drew from his (Maybold) pocket the letter to his (Maybold) friend, tore it deliberately into such minute fragments that scarcely two syllables remained in juxtaposition, and sent the whole handful of shreds fluttering into the water. Here he (Maybold) watched them eddy, dart, and turn, as they were carried downwards towards the ocean and gradually disappeared from his (Maybold) view. Finally he (Maybold) moved off, and pursued his (Maybold) way at a rapid pace back again to Mellstock Vicarage.

Nerving himself by a long and intense effort, he (Maybold) sat down in his (Maybold) study and wrote as follows:

``DEAR MISS DAY,--The meaning of your words, `the temptation is too strong,'' (he)
of your sadness and your tears, has been brought home to me by an accident. I know to-day what I did not know yesterday--that you are not a free woman.

``Why did you not tell me--why didn't you? Did you suppose I knew? No. Had I known, my conduct in coming to you as I did would have been reprehensible.

``But I don't chide you! Perhaps no blame attaches to you--I can't tell. Fancy, though my opinion of you is assailed and disturbed in a way which cannot be expressed, I love you still, and my word to you holds good yet. But will you, in justice to an honest man who relies upon your word to him (man), consider whether, under the circumstances, you can honourably forsake him (man)?--Yours ever sincerely,

``ARTHUR MAYBOLD.'' (He)

He (Maybold) rang the bell.

``Tell Charles to take these copybooks and this note to the school at once.'' (The maid)

The maid took the parcel and the letter, and in a few minutes a boy was seen to leave the vicarage gate, with the one under his (boy) arm, and the other in his (boy) hand. The vicar sat with his (vicar) hand to his (vicar) brow, watching the lad as he (vicar) descended Church Lane and entered the waterside path which intervened between that spot and the school.

Here he (vicar) was met by another boy, and after a free salutation and pugilistic frisk had passed between the two, the second boy came on his (boy) way to the vicarage, and the other vanished out of sight.

The boy came to the door, and a note for Mr. Maybold was brought in.

He (Maybold) knew the writing. Opening the envelope with an unsteady hand, he (Maybold) read the subjoined words:

``DEAR MR. MAYBOLD,--I have been thinking seriously and sadly through the whole of the night of the question you put to me last evening and of my answer. That answer, as an honest woman, I had no right to give.

``It is my nature--perhaps all women's--to love refinement of mind and manners; but even more than this, to be ever fascinated with the idea of surroundings more elegant and pleasing than those which have been customary. And you praised me, and praise is life to me. It was alone my sensations at these things which prompted my reply. Ambition and vanity they would be called; perhaps they are so.

``After this explanation I hope you will generously allow me to withdraw the answer I too hastily gave.

``And one more request. To keep the meeting of last night, and all that passed between us there, for ever a secret. Were it to become known, it would utterly blight the happiness of a trusting and generous man, whom I love still, and shall love always.--Yours sincerely,

``FANCY DAY.

The last written communication that ever passed from the vicar to Fancy, was a note containing these words only:

``Tell him (vicar) everything; it is best. He (vicar) will forgive you.'' (the vicar)

PART THE FIFTH: CONCLUSION

CHAPTER I: ``THE KNOT THERE``S NO UNTYING'

The last day of the story is dated just subsequent to that point in the development of the seasons when country people go to bed among nearly naked trees, are lulled to sleep by a fall of rain, and awake next morning among green ones; when the landscape appears embarrassed with the sudden weight and brilliancy of its leaves; when the night-jar comes and strikes up for the summer his (vicar) tune of one note; when the apple-trees have bloomed, and the roads and orchard-grass become spotted with fallen petals; when the faces of the delicate flowers are darkened, and their heads weighed down, by the throng of honey-bees, which increase their humming till humming is too mild a term for the all-pervading sound; and when cuckoos, blackbirds, and sparrows, that have hitherto been merry and respectful neighbours, become noisy and persistent intimates.

The exterior of Geoffrey Day's house in Yalbury Wood appeared exactly as was usual at that season, but a frantic barking of the dogs at the back told of unwonted movements somewhere within. Inside the door the eyes beheld a gathering, which was a rarity indeed for the dwelling of the solitary wood-steward and keeper.

About the room were sitting and standing, in various gnarled attitudes, our old acquaintance, grandfathers James and William, the tranter, Mr. Penny, two or three children, including Jimmy and Charley, besides three or four country ladies and gentlemen from a greater distance who do not require any distinction by name. Geoffrey was seen and heard stamping about the outhouse and among the bushes of the garden, attending to details of daily routine before the proper time arrived for their performance, in order that they might be off his (Geoffrey) hands for the day. He (Geoffrey) appeared with his (Geoffrey) shirt-sleeves rolled up; his (Geoffrey) best new nether garments, in which he (Geoffrey) had arrayed himself that morning, being temporarily disguised under a weekday apron whilst these proceedings were in operation. He (Geoffrey) occasionally glanced at the hives in passing, to see if his (Geoffrey) wife's bees were swarming, ultimately rolling down his (Geoffrey) shirt-sleeves and going indoors, talking to tranter Dewy whilst buttoning the wristbands, to save time; next going upstairs for his (Geoffrey) best waistcoat, and coming down again to make another remark whilst buttoning that, during the time looking fixedly in the tranter's face as if he (Geoffrey) were a looking-glass.

The furniture had undergone attenuation to an alarming extent, every duplicate piece having been removed, including the clock by Thomas Wood; Ezekiel Saunders being at last left sole referee in matters of time.

Fancy was stationary upstairs, receiving her (Geoffrey) layers of clothes and adornments, and answering by short fragments of laughter which had more fidgetiness than mirth in them, remarks that were made from time to time by Mrs. Dewy and Mrs. Penny, who were assisting her (Penny) at the toilet, Mrs. Day having pleaded a queerness in her (Penny) head as a reason for shutting herself up in an inner bedroom for the whole morning. Mrs. Penny appeared with nine corkscrew curls on each side of her (Penny) temples, and a back comb stuck upon her (Penny) crown like a castle on a steep.

The conversation just now going on was concerning the banns, the last publication of which had been on the Sunday previous.

``Well, I think I'd rather have it the way mother had it,'' (she)
she (Penny) said, and the couples moved along under the trees, every man to his (man) maid.

``Ah!'' (grandfather)
said grandfather James to grandfather William as they retired,
``I wonder which she (mother) thinks most about, Dick or her (mother) wedding raiment!'' (William/William Dewy)

``Well, `tis their nature,'' (grandfather)
said grandfather William. ``Remember the words of the prophet Jeremiah:
``Can a maid forget her (maid) ornaments, or a bride her (maid) attire?''' (unknown)

Now among dark perpendicular firs, like the shafted columns of a cathedral; now through a hazel copse, matted with primroses and wild hyacinths; now under broad beeches in bright young leaves they threaded their way into the high road over Yalbury Hill, which dipped at that point directly into the village of Geoffrey Day's parish; and in the space of a quarter of an hour Fancy found herself to be Mrs. Richard Dewy, though, much to her (maid) surprise, feeling no other than Fancy Day still.

On the circuitous return walk through the lanes and fields, amid much chattering and laughter, especially when they came to stiles, Dick discerned a brown spot far up a turnip field.

``Why, `tis Enoch!'' (he)
he (Dick) said to Fancy.
``I thought I missed him (Enoch) at the house this morning. How is it he (Enoch)'s left you?'' (unknown)

``He (Enoch) drank too much cider, and it got into his (Enoch) head, and they put him (Enoch) in Weatherbury stocks for it. Father was obliged to get somebody else for a day or two, and Enoch hasn't had anything to do with the woods since.'' (unknown)

``We might ask him (Enoch) to call down to-night. Stocks are nothing for once, considering `tis our wedding day.'' (The party)
The bridal party was ordered to halt.

``Eno-o-o-o-ch!'' (Dick/Dick Dewy/DICK)
cried Dick at the top of his (Dick) voice.

``Y-a-a-a-a-a-as!'' (Enoch)
said Enoch from the distance.

``D'ye know who I be-e-e-e-e-e?'' (unknown)

``No-o-o-o-o-o-o!'' (unknown)

``Dick Dew-w-w-w-wy!'' (unknown)

``O-h-h-h-h-h!'' (unknown)

``Just a-ma-a-a-a-a-arried!'' (unknown)

``O-h-h-h-h-h!'' (unknown)

``This is my wife, Fa-a-a-a-a-ancy!'' (Enoch)
-LRB-holding her (maid) up to Enoch's view as if she (maid) had been a nosegay.-RRB-

``O-h-h-h-h-h!'' (unknown)

``Will ye come across to the party to-ni-i-i-i-i-i-ight!'' (unknown)

``Ca-a-a-a-a-an`t!'' (unknown)

``Why n-o-o-o-o-ot?'' (unknown)

``Don't work for the family no-o-o-o-ow!'' (unknown)

``Not nice of Master Enoch,'' (Dick/Dick Dewy/DICK)
said Dick, as they resumed their walk.

``You mustn't blame en,'' (Geoffrey/Geoffrey Day)
said Geoffrey;
``the man's not hisself now; he (man)'s in his (man) morning frame of mind. When he (man)'s had a gallon o' cider or ale, or a pint or two of mead, the man's well enough, and his (man) manners be as good as anybody's in the kingdom.'' (Geoffrey/Geoffrey Day)

CHAPTER II: UNDER THE GREENWOOD TREE

The point in Yalbury Wood which abutted on the end of Geoffrey Day's premises was closed with an ancient tree, horizontally of enormous extent, though having no great pretensions to height. Many hundreds of birds had been born amidst the boughs of this single tree; tribes of rabbits and hares had nibbled at its bark from year to year; quaint tufts of fungi had sprung from the cavities of its forks; and countless families of moles and earthworms had crept about its roots. Beneath and beyond its shade spread a carefully-tended grass-plot, its purpose being to supply a healthy exercise-ground for young chickens and pheasants; the hens, their mothers, being enclosed in coops placed upon the same green flooring.

All these encumbrances were now removed, and as the afternoon advanced, the guests gathered on the spot, where music, dancing, and the singing of songs went forward with great spirit throughout the evening. The propriety of every one was intense by reason of the influence of Fancy, who, as an additional precaution in this direction, had strictly charged her (their) father and the tranter to carefully avoid saying `thee' and `thou' in their conversation, on the plea that those ancient words sounded so very humiliating to persons of newer taste; also that they were never to be seen drawing the back of the hand across the mouth after drinking--a local English custom of extraordinary antiquity, but stated by Fancy to be decidedly dying out among the better classes of society.

In addition to the local musicians present, a man who had a thorough knowledge of the tambourine was invited from the village of Tantrum Clangley,--a place long celebrated for the skill of its inhabitants as performers on instruments of percussion. These important members of the assembly were relegated to a height of two or three feet from the ground, upon a temporary erection of planks supported by barrels. Whilst the dancing progressed the older persons sat in a group under the trunk of the tree,--the space being allotted to them somewhat grudgingly by the young ones, who were greedy of pirouetting room,--and fortified by a table against the heels of the dancers. Here the gaffers and gammers, whose dancing days were over, told stories of great impressiveness, and at intervals surveyed the advancing and retiring couples from the same retreat, as people on shore might be supposed to survey a naval engagement in the bay beyond; returning again to their tales when the pause was over. Those of the whirling throng, who, during the rests between each figure, turned their eyes in the direction of these seated ones, were only able to discover, on account of the music and bustle, that a very striking circumstance was in course of narration--denoted by an emphatic sweep of the hand, snapping of the fingers, close of the lips, and fixed look into the centre of the listener's eye for the space of a quarter of a minute, which raised in that listener such a reciprocating working of face as to sometimes make the distant dancers half wish to know what such an interesting tale could refer to.

Fancy caused her (listener) looks to wear as much matronly expression as was obtainable out of six hours' experience as a wife, in order that the contrast between her (wife) own state of life and that of the unmarried young women present might be duly impressed upon the company: occasionally stealing glances of admiration at her (wife) left hand, but this quite privately; for her (wife) ostensible bearing concerning the matter was intended to show that, though she (wife) undoubtedly occupied the most wondrous position in the eyes of the world that had ever been attained, she (wife) was almost unconscious of the circumstance, and that the somewhat prominent position in which that wonderfully-emblazoned left hand was continually found to be placed, when handing cups and saucers, knives, forks, and glasses, was quite the result of accident. As to wishing to excite envy in the bosoms of her (wife) maiden companions, by the exhibition of the shining ring, every one was to know it was quite foreign to the dignity of such an experienced married woman. Dick's imagination in the meantime was far less capable of drawing so much wontedness from his (Dick) new condition. He (Dick) had been for two or three hours trying to feel himself merely a newly- married man, but had been able to get no further in the attempt than to realize that he (man) was Dick Dewy, the tranter's son, at a party given by Lord Wessex's head man-in-charge, on the outlying Yalbury estate, dancing and chatting with Fancy Day.

Five country dances, including

``Haste to the Wedding,'' (unknown)
two reels, and three fragments of horn-pipes, brought them to the time for supper, which, on account of the dampness of the grass from the immaturity of the summer season, was spread indoors. At the conclusion of the meal Dick went out to put the horse in; and Fancy, with the elder half of the four bridesmaids, retired upstairs to dress for the journey to Dick's new cottage near Mellstock.

``How long will you be putting on your bonnet, Fancy?'' (Dick/Dick Dewy/DICK)
Dick inquired at the foot of the staircase. Being now a man of business and married, he (Dick) was strong on the importance of time, and doubled the emphasis of his (Dick) words in conversing, and added vigour to his (Dick) nods.

``Only a minute.'' (unknown)

``How long is that?'' (unknown)

``Well, dear, five.'' (unknown)

``Ah, sonnies!'' (Dick/Dick Dewy/DICK)
said the tranter, as Dick retired,
''`tis a talent of the female race that low numbers should stand for high, more especially in matters of waiting, matters of age, and matters of money.'' (Dick/Dick Dewy/DICK)

``True, true, upon my body,'' (Geoffrey/Geoffrey Day)
said Geoffrey.

``Ye spak with feeling, Geoffrey, seemingly.'' (unknown)

``Anybody that d'know my experience might guess that.'' (unknown)

``What's she (Geoffrey) doing now, Geoffrey?'' (unknown)

``Claning out all the upstairs drawers and cupboards, and dusting the second-best chainey--a thing that's only done once a year. ``If there's work to be done I must do it,

'' says she (Dick), `wedding or no.''' (I)

''`Tis my belief she (Dick)'s a very good woman at bottom.'' (I)

``She (Geoffrey)'s terrible deep, then.'' (Mrs. Penny)

Mrs. Penny turned round.

``Well, `tis humps and hollers with the best of us; but still and for all that, Dick and Fancy stand as fair a chance of having a bit of sunsheen as any married pair in the land.'' (unknown)

``Ay, there's no gainsaying it.'' (Mrs. Dewy)

Mrs. Dewy came up, talking to one person and looking at another.

``Happy, yes,'' (she)
she (Dewy) said.
''`Tis always so when a couple is so exactly in tune with one another as Dick and she (Dewy).'' (she)

``When they be'n't too poor to have time to sing,'' (grandfather)
said grandfather James.

``I tell ye, neighbours, when the pinch comes,'' (unknown)
said the tranter:
``when the oldest daughter's boots be only a size less than her (daughter) mother's, and the rest o' the flock close behind her (daughter). A sharp time for a man that, my sonnies; a very sharp time! Chanticleer's comb is a-cut then, 'a believe.'' (unknown)

``That's about the form o`t,'' (Mr. Penny/Penny)
said Mr. Penny.
``That'll put the stuns upon a man, when you must measure mother and daughter's lasts to tell 'em apart.'' (unknown)

``You've no cause to complain, Reuben, of such a close-coming flock,'' (unknown)
said Mrs. Dewy;
``for ours was a straggling lot enough, God knows!'' (unknown)

``I d'know it, I d'know it,'' (unknown)
said the tranter.
``You be a well-enough woman, Ann.'' (Mrs. Dewy)

Mrs. Dewy put her (Dewy) mouth in the form of a smile, and put it back again without smiling.

``And if they come together, they go together,'' (Mrs. Penny)
said Mrs. Penny, whose family had been the reverse of the tranter's;
``and a little money will make either fate tolerable. And money can be made by our young couple, I know.'' (family)

``Yes, that it can!'' (Leaf)
said the impulsive voice of Leaf, who had hitherto humbly admired the proceedings from a corner.
``It can be done--all that's wanted is a few pounds to begin with. That's all! I know a story about it!'' (unknown)

``Let's hear thy story, Leaf,'' (unknown)
said the tranter.
``I never knew you were clever enough to tell a story. Silence, all of ye! Mr. Leaf will tell a story.'' (unknown)

``Tell your story, Thomas Leaf,'' (grandfather)
said grandfather William in the tone of a schoolmaster.

``Once,'' (the Leaf)
said the delighted Leaf, in an uncertain voice, ``there was a man who lived in a house! Well, this man went thinking and thinking night and day. At last, he (man) said to himself, as I might, ``If I had only ten pound, I'd make a fortune.
'' At last by hook or by crook, behold he (crook) got the ten pounds!'' (I)

``Only think of that!'' (Nat Callcome/Nat)
said Nat Callcome satirically.

``Silence!'' (unknown)
said the tranter.

``Well, now comes the interesting part of the story! In a little time he (man) made that ten pounds twenty. Then a little time after that he (man) doubled it, and made it forty. Well, he (man) went on, and a good while after that he (man) made it eighty, and on to a hundred. Well, by-and-by he (man) made it two hundred! Well, you'd never believe it, but--he (man) went on and made it four hundred! He (man) went on, and what did he (man) do? Why, he (man) made it eight hundred! Yes, he (man) did,'' (Leaf)
continued Leaf, in the highest pitch of excitement, bringing down his (crook) fist upon his (crook) knee with such force that he (crook) quivered with the pain;
``yes, and he (man) went on and made it A THOUSAND!'' (he)

``Hear, hear!'' (unknown)
said the tranter.
``Better than the history of England, my sonnies!'' (unknown)

``Thank you for your story, Thomas Leaf,'' (grandfather)
said grandfather William; and then Leaf gradually sank into nothingness again.

Amid a medley of laughter, old shoes, and elder-wine, Dick and his (Dick) bride took their departure, side by side in the excellent new spring-cart which the young tranter now possessed. The moon was just over the full, rendering any light from lamps or their own beauties quite unnecessary to the pair. They drove slowly along Yalbury Bottom, where the road passed between two copses. Dick was talking to his (Dick) companion.

``Fancy,'' (he)
he (Dick) said,
``why we are so happy is because there is such full confidence between us. Ever since that time you confessed to that little flirtation with Shiner by the river -LRB-which was really no flirtation at all-RRB-, I have thought how artless and good you must be to tell me o' such a trifling thing, and to be so frightened about it as you were. It has won me to tell you my every deed and word since then. We'll have no secrets from each other, darling, will we ever?--no secret at all.'' (he)

``None from to-day,'' (unknown)
said Fancy.
``Hark! what's that?'' (unknown)

From a neighbouring thicket was suddenly heard to issue in a loud, musical, and liquid voice--

``Tippiwit! swe-e-et! ki-ki-ki! Come hither, come hither, come hither!'' (unknown)

``O, `tis the nightingale,'' (she)
murmured she (bride), and thought of a secret she (bride) would never tell.
